أثراً لا يقدح في ذلك فلا يتم التقريب . قوله : ( نفي (محض) لا تميز له أصلاً . قوله : ( فلا يصلح الخ) إذ الصلاحية فرع الامتياز، لا لأنه يلزم جمع النقيضين، لأن ذلك : في كونه أثراً لا في صلاحيته، فلا يكون هذا الوجه راجعاً إلى الأول كما وهم . قوله : ( ولأنه أعني الأثر حال عدمه إلخ إرجاع الضمير إلى الأثر المفهوم من التأثير لا إلى المذكور صريحاً، دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد بأن الكلام في التأثير بمعنى الإيجاد، وإلا لما صح أن التأثير حال الوجود إيجاد للوجود ، وحال العدم جمع للنقيضين، فالقول بأن العدم نفي صرف لا يصلح أثراً ليس كما ينبغي، لكن لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه حينئذ كما نقل من الشارح راجع إلى الوجه السابق عليه إذ ماله إلى اجتماع النقيضين ولو ذكر هذا الوجه في نفي التأثير في العدم حال العدم لكان وجها مستقلا، إذ العدم نفي محض لا يصلح لتأثر الموثر مطلقاً . قوله : ( على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به تأثير وإيجاد في هذا التقرير دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد بأن الوجه الثالث ليس بتام؛ لأن العدم ربما كان حادثاً لا مستمراً. ووجه الدفع أن العدم الحادث يصدق عليه أنه مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق بالأثر إيجاد، وإن لم يصدق أنه مستمر بمعنى أنه غير مسبوق بالوجود ، وليس المراد بالاستمرار المعنى الثاني، كما توهم المعترض. التأثير في العدم قلت : التأثير إما فيه حال كون الأثر معدوماً، وهو تحصيل الحاصل وإما حال كونه موجوداً وأنه جمع للنقيضين، وأيضاً هو حال الوجود لا يصلح أثراً للمعدوم، وأيضاً هو حينئذ مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به الإعدام فلا يستند إلى مؤثر العدم.
Something in something but it is not intelligible; for influence (al-ta'thīr) in existence, for example, is either in the state of existence, i.e., the existence of the effect, and it is impossible because it is the creation of the existent (ījād al-mawjūd) and obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), or in the state of non-existence, and it is also false because it is a combination of opposites (jamʿ al-naqīḍayn). This is because the existence of the effect together with influence does not lag behind it at all, like breaking with breaking, and existence with creation. When it is assumed that influence in existence, namely creation, is only in the state of non-existence, then the existence of the effect is also in that state, so the existence of the effect and its non-existence come together. And because it (i.e., the effect in the state of its non-existence) is pure negation, it is not suitable in this state to be an effect for the agent, and since there is no effect for him, there is no influence and no creation from him then. And because it (i.e., the effect in the state of its non-existence) is continuous upon what it was before being connected to influence and creation, it is not attributed, while being continuous upon its prior state before creation, to an agent of existence. Thus the claim of influence in existence in the state of non-existence is refuted in three ways. If you wish, it is a negation that reason allows, not that it conceives it; otherwise it would not be possible to refute it. And if reason does not allow influence, the need for an agent insofar as it is an agent cannot be established. His statement: 'in existence' and the evidence for this specification is his statement: 'because it is the creation of the existent' and his statement: 'because of the two opposites' – for if influence were in non-existence, the matter would be the opposite. His statement: 'i.e., the effect etc.' means that the pronoun refers to the effect understood from what preceded, not to non-existence, because the discussion is about influence in existence in the state of non-existence of the effect, and the fact that non-existence is not suitable to be an effect does not harm that, so the approximation is not complete. His statement: 'pure negation' has no distinction at all. His statement: 'so it is not suitable etc.' because suitability is a branch of distinction, not because it entails combination of opposites, for that is in its being an effect, not in its suitability, so this aspect is not reducible to the first as was imagined. His statement: 'and because it, I mean the effect in the state of its non-existence etc.' – returning the pronoun to the effect understood from influence, not to what is explicitly mentioned, is a refutation of the objection of the commentator of al-Maqāṣid that the discussion is about influence in the sense of creation; otherwise it would not be correct that influence in the state of existence is creation of existence, and in the state of non-existence is combination of opposites. So the statement that non-existence is pure negation not suitable to be an effect is not as it should be. However, it is not hidden that this aspect then, as narrated from the commentator, is reducible to the previous aspect, for it leads to combination of opposites. And if this aspect were mentioned in denying influence in non-existence in the state of non-existence, it would be an independent aspect, for non-existence is pure negation not suitable for being affected by an agent at all. His statement: 'upon what it was before being connected to influence and creation' – in this exposition is a refutation of the objection of the commentator of al-Maqāṣid that the third aspect is not complete because non-existence might be transient, not continuous. The refutation is that transient non-existence is described as continuous upon what it was before being connected to the effect by creation, even if it is not described as continuous in the sense of being un preceded by existence; and the intended meaning of continuity is not the second meaning, as the objector imagined. Influence in non-existence: I say: influence is either in it in the state of the effect being non-existent, and it is obtaining what is already obtained, or in the state of it being existent, and it is combination of opposites, and also it is in the state of existence not suitable as an effect for the non-existent, and also it is then continuous upon what it was before being connected to non-existence, so it is not attributed to an agent of non-existence.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary presenting and explaining three arguments (wujūh) that demonstrate the impossibility of influence (ta'thīr) on something that exists at the time when it is non-existent. The author begins by stating that the notion of 'something in something' is unintelligible. He then lays out the three arguments: First, if the influence (here meaning creation, ījād) occurs while the effect already exists, it is impossible because it would be creating what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd), i.e., obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). Second, if the influence occurs while the effect is non-existent, it would combine existence and non-existence simultaneously, since the effect would exist by virtue of the influence and yet be non-existent. Third, the effect in its state of non-existence is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), so it cannot serve as an effect; furthermore, it is continuous in its state and thus cannot be attributed to an agent of existence. The commentator then provides detailed glosses on each part, clarifying pronoun references, defending against objections from other commentators (especially the commentator of al-Maqāṣid), and explaining why certain interpretations are misguided. He concludes by summarizing the three ways and noting that the non-existence of the effect is not suitable for being affected.

Key Terms

الإيجاد
الوجود
العدم
المحال
جمع النقيضين
نفي محض
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three proofs are completely independent and distinct.
Correction: The third proof is actually reducible to the second (combination of opposites), as the commentary indicates.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given against influence in existence at the state of non-existence?
A) It is combination of opposites.
B) It is obtaining what is already obtained.
C) The effect is pure negation.
D) The effect is continuous.

Key Takeaway
An agent cannot influence a thing while it is non-existent, because it would either be creating the existent or combining opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Causality and Created Things

Distinguishing Prior and Concurrent Existence in Creation
Answer distinguishing two types of existence in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between prior and concurrent existence and why only creation with prior existence is impossible.

Core Idea
Creation is impossible only when existence precedes the act; concurrent existence is perfectly possible.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that seemed to make all creation impossible. He clarifies that the impossibility is limited to a specific case: creating something that already exists with a prior existence. He then shows that if the objection were valid, it would negate all observable changes in the world, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the main answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the two cases: prior existence (impossible) and concurrent existence (possible).
Notice the phrase 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is obtained).
2
Understand the impossibility of prior existence
The author explains that creating something with a prior existence is impossible because it would be obtaining what is already obtained, which is self-evidently absurd.
3
See the concurrent existence case
The author provides an analogy: the movement of the hand (cause) and the movement of the key (effect) occur simultaneously, so obtaining the effect at the same time as the cause is not impossible.
4
Follow the refutation
If the opponent's generalization were true, no accidental attribute (like heat or sound) could ever be generated, because its generation would face the same dilemma. But we observe such attributes, so the argument fails.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Note the intuitive proof
The occurrence of attributes and their dependence on a cause is intuitive (badīhī), so the opponent's argument is definitively refuted.
Even if the exact cause is disputed, the fact of occurrence is undeniable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن المحال إيجاد ما هو موجود بوجود قبل) أي قبل الإيجاد فإنه تحصيل لما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التحصيل، وهو محال بديهة. ( وإلا فالإيجاد للموجود ) بوجود مقارن للإيجاد ؛ لأن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، وذلك تحصيل للحاصل بهذا التحصيل ولا استحالة فيه. ( ولو صح ما ذكرتم لزم أن لا يحدث (صفة) في نفسها ( أصلا ، كهذه السخونة وهذا الصوت )؛ لأن حدوثها إما حال أو عدمها وهو اجتماع النقيضين أعني الوجود والعدم وإما حال وجودها وهو حصول الحاصل. نقول : لزم أن لا يحدث صفة في شيء من مؤثر يحدثها؛ لأن إحداثها وإيجادها إما حال الوجود أو العدم، وكلاهما باطل، لكن حدوث هذه الصفات واستنادها إلى أمر يحدثها أمر بديهي فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً. والحل : أن قوله : ( لا يصلح أثراً للمعدوم لأنه موجود وأثر المعدوم يكون معدوماً. قوله : ( حينئذ) ظرف لنفي الأثر والتأثير على التنازع . قوله : (مستمر على ما كان (عليه لأن المفروض أن التأثير في الوجود حال العدم السابق على الوجود وليس المراد بالمستمر العدم الذي لا ابتداء له، إذ لا يتعلق غرضنا بكونه أزلياً، ولا يتوقف نفي الإيجاد حال العدم عليه . قوله : ( أن المحال (الخ أي المحال مقصور على هذا الإيجاد. قوله : ( وهو محال بديهة إذ لا يكون التحصيل حينئذ تحصيلاً. قوله : ( وألا) (الخ) أي لا يكون المحال مقصوراً على هذا الإيجاد لم يصح القول باستحالة إيجاد الموجود بوجود مقارن للإيجاد، لا استحالة فيه بناء على أن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، كما يشاهد ذلك في حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح. وإذا تقرر ذلك فنقول : إن أراد المستدل من إيجاد الموجود النوع الأول منعنا الملازمة، لكونه إيجاداً للموجود بهذا الوجود، وإن أراد الثاني أو الأعم نمنع بطلان التالي لأن المحال هو النوع الأول. ولما كان سند المنعين المذكورين مستفاداً من تلك المقدمة تعرض المجيب لبيانها واكتفى بها لانسياق الذهن إلى المنعين المذكورين منها بلا كلفة، فتدبر، فإنه قد تحير في حل هذه العبارة الناظرون . قوله : ( بديهي) وإن اختلف في تعيين ذلك المؤثر المحدث . قوله : ( فانتقض الخ لاستلزامه المحال وهو الحكم بخلاف ما تشهد به البديهة. قوله : ( أما حال كون الأثر معدوماً) المراد من الأثر هاهنا هو الماهية الممكنة باعتبار العدم لا العدم نفسه، كما أن المراد بالأثر سابقاً هو تلك الماهية باعتبار الوجود لا الوجود نفسه، فلا يرد أن معدومية الأثر الذي هو العدم يستلزم الوجود، فلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل كما ظن .
The answer: That which is impossible is the creation of what is existent with a prior existence (bi-wujūd qabl), i.e., before creation, for it is obtaining what was already obtained before this obtaining, and it is impossible by intuition. Otherwise, the creation of the existent with an existence concurrent with creation (bi-wujūd muqārin li-l-ījād) is not impossible, because the occurrence of the effect together with influence is temporal, and that is obtaining what is obtained by this obtaining, and there is no impossibility in it. And if what you mentioned were correct, then it would follow that no attribute would ever occur in itself, like this heat and this sound; because their occurrence is either in the state of its non-existence, which is the combination of opposites – namely existence and non-existence – or in the state of its existence, which is obtaining what is already obtained. We say: It would follow that no attribute would occur in anything from an agent that causes it, because its causing and creation are either in the state of existence or non-existence, and both are false; but the occurrence of these attributes and their attribution to something that causes them is intuitive, so your argument is definitively refuted. The solution: That his statement: 'it is not suitable as an effect for the non-existent because it is existent, and the effect of the non-existent is non-existent.' His statement: 'then' is an adverb for negating the effect and influence in a way that they compete. His statement: 'continuous upon what it was' because it is assumed that influence in existence is in the state of non-existence prior to existence, and by 'continuous' is not meant the non-existence that has no beginning, for our purpose does not depend on its being eternal, nor does the negation of creation in the state of non-existence depend on it. His statement: 'that which is impossible etc.' i.e., the impossible is limited to this creation. His statement: 'and it is impossible by intuition' because the obtaining then is not an obtaining. His statement: 'otherwise etc.' i.e., if the impossible were not limited to this creation, then the statement that it is impossible to create the existent with an existence concurrent with creation would not be valid; there is no impossibility in it based on the fact that the effect occurs with influence temporally, as is seen in the movement of the hand and the movement of the key. And when this is established, we say: if the arguer means by 'creation of the existent' the first type, we deny the implication, because it is creation of the existent with this existence; and if he means the second type or the general, we deny the falsehood of the consequent, because the impossible is only the first type. And since the basis of the two mentioned denials is derived from that premise, the respondent undertook to clarify it and sufficed with it for the mind to be led to the two mentioned denials from it without difficulty. So ponder, for the observers have been perplexed in solving this expression. His statement: 'intuitive' – even if they differ in specifying that causing agent. His statement: 'so it is refuted etc.' because it entails what is impossible, namely judging contrary to what intuition testifies to. His statement: 'As for the state of the effect being non-existent' – what is meant by 'effect' here is the possible quiddity considered as non-existent, not non-existence itself, just as what was meant by 'effect' previously was that quiddity considered as existent, not existence itself. So it does not follow that the non-existence of the effect which is non-existence entails existence, so obtaining what is already obtained does not necessarily follow, as was imagined.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawāb) to the objection raised by the earlier arguments. The author begins by clarifying that the impossibility (muḥāl) is specifically limited to the creation of something that already exists with a prior existence (wujūd qabl), because that would be obtaining what is already obtained. However, creating something with an existence that is concurrent with the act of creation (wujūd muqārin) is not impossible, because the effect and the influence occur simultaneously in time, like the movement of the hand causing the movement of a key. The author then argues that if the opponent's reasoning were sound, it would entail that no accidental attributes (like heat or sound) could ever be generated, since their emergence would be subject to the same dilemma. But these attributes do occur, and their dependence on a cause is intuitive (badīhī), so the argument is self-refuting. The rest of the commentary explains the nuances of the phrasing, clarifies the referents of pronouns, and defends the answer against potential misunderstandings. A key distinction is drawn between the effect considered as a possible quiddity (māhiyya mumkina) under the aspect of non-existence versus under the aspect of existence, thereby avoiding the charge of obtaining what is already obtained.

Correction: The answer shows it is only impossible when the existence precedes the act of creation; concurrent existence is perfectly possible.

Influence and the Two Types of Existence
Objection and answer regarding causal influence in non-existence or existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against causal influence and the answer that distinguishes between preceding and concomitant existence.

Core Idea
Causal influence is impossible only if the effect's existence precedes the act.

This passage presents a critical objection to the possibility of causal influence: if the effect is non-existent, influence would be obtaining the obtained; if it is existent, influence would combine opposites. The answer resolves this by distinguishing between existence that precedes the act (impossible) and existence that is simultaneous (possible).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the two cases: effect non-existent and effect existent. Understand why each is problematic according to the objector.
The term 'tahsil al-hasil' is key.
2
Examine the additional arguments
The objector adds that in existence, the effect is not suitable for a non-existent cause and it continues as before.
3
Study the answer's distinction
The answer clarifies that the impossibility applies only to existence that precedes the act, not to simultaneous existence.
Look for 'wujud qabl' and 'wujud muqarin'.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the answer avoids the objection and why this distinction is important for establishing causal agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : التأثير إما فيه حال كون الأثر معدوماً، وهو تحصيل الحاصل وإما حال كونه موجوداً وأنه جمع للنقيضين، وأيضاً هو حال الوجود لا يصلح أثراً للمعدوم، وأيضاً هو حينئذ مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به الإعدام فلا يستند إلى مؤثر العدم. ( والجواب : أن المحال إيجاد ما هو موجود بوجود قبل) أي قبل الإيجاد فإنه تحصيل لما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التحصيل، وهو محال بديهة. ( وإلا فالإيجاد للموجود ) بوجود مقارن للإيجاد ؛ لأن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، وذلك تحصيل للحاصل بهذا التحصيل ولا استحالة فيه.
I said: Influence is either in the state of the effect being non-existent – which is obtaining the obtained – or in the state of it being existent – and that is combining the two opposites. Also, in the state of existence it is not suitable as an effect for the non-existent, and also it is then continuing as it was before being connected to non-existence, so it is not attributed to the agent of non-existence. (The answer: That the impossible is bringing into existence what is existent with an existence that is before) i.e., before the bringing into existence, for it is obtaining what was already obtained before this obtaining, and that is self-evidently impossible. (Otherwise, bringing into existence is for the existent) with an existence concomitant with the bringing into existence, because obtaining the effect with the influence is simultaneous, and that is obtaining the obtained by this obtaining, and there is no impossibility in it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the possibility of causal influence (ta'thīr) on a contingent being, followed by its answer. The objector argues that influence cannot occur in the state of non-existence of the effect because that would be 'obtaining the obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), nor in the state of existence because that would combine two opposites (jam' al-naqīḍayn). Additionally, in the state of existence the effect is already existent and thus not suitable as an effect for a non-existent cause, and it would be continuing as before, so it cannot be attributed to an agent of non-existence. The answer distinguishes between two types of existence: existence that precedes the act of bringing into existence (ījād) – which would indeed be obtaining the obtained and is impossible – and existence that is concomitant with the act (wujūd muqārin li-l-ījād), i.e., simultaneous, which does not entail impossibility because the effect comes into being together with the influence.

Key Terms
تأثير
تحصيل الحاصل
جمع النقيضين
وجود قبل
وجود مقارن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any act of bringing into existence something already existent is impossible.
Correction: It is only impossible if the existence precedes the act; simultaneous existence is not impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what kind of existence makes bringing into existence impossible?
A) Existence that precedes the act of bringing into existence
B) Existence that is simultaneous with the act
C) Both types of existence
D) Neither type of existence

Key Takeaway
Causal influence is impossible only if the effect's existence precedes the act; simultaneous existence with the act is not problematic.

This lesson teaches that causal influence is not bound by temporal precedence. How can you live out this truth today?
?
The objector argued that influence is either in non-existence or existence, both absurd. But the answer reveals a third possibility: existence with the act. What are some areas in your faith where you have fallen into a similar false dichotomy, and how can you embrace the deeper truth that avoids both extremes?

Causality and Created Things

The Self-Evident Arising of Attributes
Objection that attributes cannot arise, refuted by self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent's argument against the possibility of attribute generation is refuted by appealing to self-evident experience.

Core Idea
The arising of attributes is self-evident; no logical trick can overturn what we directly experience.

The opponent extends the earlier argument to claim that no attribute can ever arise, because generation would require a logical impossibility. The answer cuts straight to experience: we directly observe attributes like heat and sound arising, and we know they depend on a cause. This immediate self-evident knowledge refutes the abstract proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the objection and the answer.
Notice the structure: the opponent's premise, the dilemma, the conclusion, then the refutation.
2
Analyze the opponent's dilemma
The opponent argues that an attribute cannot arise because its arising would either be in a state of non-existence (contradiction) or in a state of existence (redundancy). Understand why both horns are considered impossible.
3
See the answer's appeal to self-evidence
The response does not counter the logic directly but says the arising of attributes is self-evident (badīhī). Reflect on what 'self-evident' means in this context.
In kalam, self-evident truths are foundational and need no proof.
4
Recognize the method of refutation
The refutation works by showing that the opponent's conclusion contradicts direct observation, which is a stronger epistemic authority. This is a common strategy in Islamic theology.
The phrase 'فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً' indicates a definitive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولو صح ما ذكرتم لزم أن لا يحدث (صفة) في نفسها ( أصلا ، كهذه السخونة وهذا الصوت ) ؛ لأن حدوثها إما حال أو عدمها وهو اجتماع النقيضين أعني الوجود والعدم وإما حال وجودها وهو حصول الحاصل. نقول : لزم أن لا يحدث صفة في شيء من مؤثر يحدثها؛ لأن إحداثها وإيجادها إما حال الوجود أو العدم، وكلاهما باطل، لكن حدوث هذه الصفات واستنادها إلى أمر يحدثها أمر بديهي فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً.
(And if what you mentioned were correct, it would entail that no attribute arises in itself at all, such as this heat and this sound; because their arising is either in the state of its non-existence – which is combining the two opposites, namely existence and non-existence – or in the state of its existence – which is obtaining the obtained. We say: It would entail that no attribute arises in anything from any agent that brings it about, because its bringing about and bringing into existence is either in the state of existence or non-existence, and both are invalid. But the arising of these attributes and their being attributed to something that brings them about is self-evident, so your proof is refuted definitively.)
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection extending the first argument to the case of attributes (ṣifāt) such as heat and sound. The opponent argues that if the previous reasoning were correct, then no attribute could ever arise, because its arising would either be in the state of its non-existence (combining opposites) or in the state of its existence (obtaining the obtained). The answer is a simple refutation: the arising of attributes and their dependence on a cause is self-evident (badīhī), so the opponent's proof is contradicted by manifest experience. The commentator notes that this refutation is valid because the opponent's argument leads to an absurd conclusion that contradicts self-evident truth.

Key Terms

حدوث
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's argument is logically airtight and must be refuted by equally logical reasoning.
Correction: The refutation appeals to self-evident experience (badīhī), which is a legitimate and stronger epistemic ground in kalam. Direct observation overrides abstract logical dilemmas.

Checkpoint

A) By showing it leads to a contradiction
B) By appealing to self-evident experience
C) By distinguishing types of existence
D) By denying the premise that attributes arise

Key Takeaway
The arising of attributes like heat and sound is directly observed and therefore cannot be denied by an abstract logical dilemma.

Necessity Conditional on the Predicate: The Final Solution to the Causality Objection
Sanusi provides the final solution: the impossibility of influence is conditional, not absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'necessity conditional on the predicate' (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl) and how it resolves the objection that a cause cannot influence an effect in either its existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
Impossibility conditional on a state does not negate intrinsic possibility.

The objection that a cause cannot influence an effect because influence is impossible in both the state of existence and non-existence seems to challenge causality. The final solution presented here introduces a key distinction: the impossibility is conditional on the predicate (existence or non-existence), not an absolute impossibility. This lesson unpacks that solution, showing how it preserves the intrinsic possibility of the contingent being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the technical term 'ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl' (necessity conditional on the predicate). This is the core concept.
Pay attention to how the passage distinguishes conditional necessity from absolute necessity.
2
Understand conditional necessity vs. intrinsic possibility
The passage explains that the impossibility of influence under the condition of existence or non-existence is a conditional necessity. This does not negate intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), which considers the essence alone without any additional attributes.
Intrinsic possibility is about the essence itself, not under any condition.
3
Analyze the reformulation of the objection
The passage reinterprets the opponent's premise: if 'influence is either in existence or non-existence' means conditional on those states, the exclusive disjunction is false. If it means in the time of existence or non-existence, it is possible. This shows the solution's scope.
The reformulation clarifies that the objection only works if you assume a condition, which is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحل : أن ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من استحالة التأثير حال الوجود أو حال العدم، ضرورة بشرط المحمول، فإن التأثير في وجود الأثر بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم محال، فسلب التأثير في الوجود مثلا ضروري بشرط اتصاف الأثر بالوجود أو العدم، ومثل ذلك يسمى ضرورة بشرط المحمول، (وهو) أي هذا المذكور أعني الضرورة المشروطة بالمحمول ( لا ينافي الإمكان (الذاتي ) ؛ لأن الملاحظ فيه الذات دون مالها من الصفات، فامتناع التأثير بشرط إحدى هاتين الصفتين لا ينافي إمكانه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن في زمان كل واحدة منهما وتحريره أن يقال قولك التأثير إما حال الوجود أو حال العدم وكلاهما باطل إن أردت به التأثير إما بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم فالحصر ممنوع فإن التأثير في ذات الممكن من حيث هو لا بشرط الوجود، ولا بشرط العدم، وإن أردت به أنه في زمان الوجود أو زمان العدم اخترنا أنه في زمان
And the solution: That what you mentioned – the impossibility of influence in the state of existence or the state of non-existence – is a necessity conditional on the predicate (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl). For influence in the existence of the effect conditional on existence or conditional on non-existence is impossible. So the negation of influence in existence, for example, is necessary conditional on the effect being characterized by existence or non-existence. And that like is called 'necessity conditional on the predicate'. (And it) i.e., this mentioned, namely the necessity conditioned by the predicate, (does not negate intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī)), because what is considered therein is the essence without its attributes. So the impossibility of influence conditional on one of these two attributes does not negate its possibility with respect to the essence of the contingent in the time of each of them. And its formulation is that your saying 'influence is either in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence, and both are invalid' – if you mean by it influence either conditional on existence or conditional on non-existence, then the exclusive disjunction is denied, because influence is in the essence of the contingent as such, not conditional on existence and not conditional on non-existence. And if you mean by it that it is in the time of existence or the time of non-existence, we choose that it is in the time...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage provides the final, complete solution to the original objection. It explains that the impossibility of influence in the state of existence or non-existence is a 'necessity conditional on the predicate' (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl). This means that the impossibility is only when the effect is considered under the condition of being existent or non-existent. Such a conditional necessity does not negate the intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) of the contingent being, because intrinsic possibility considers the essence (dhāt) alone, without any additional attributes. Therefore, the exclusive disjunction (al-ḥaṣr) between influence in existence or non-existence is false if taken with the condition. The correct view is that influence occurs in the essence of the contingent as such, without any condition, and can be in the time of existence or non-existence. The commentator adds that this solution cuts off the root of the doubt.

Key Terms
ضرورة بشرط المحمول
إمكان ذاتي
حصر
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of influence in existence or non-existence is absolute, negating causality entirely.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the impossibility is conditional on the predicate (existence or non-existence), not an intrinsic impossibility of the essence. Intrinsic possibility remains, so causality is not negated.

Checkpoint

C) The self-evident arising of attributes
D) The simultaneous existence of cause and effect

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of influence under the condition of existence or non-existence does not undermine the intrinsic possibility of the contingent, because intrinsic possibility considers the essence alone.

Second Doubt: The Impossibility of Influence and Its Answer
States and refutes the second doubt that influence is impossible in three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second doubt against the need for an influencer for the possible, its threefold argument, and the correct answer that influence is on particular existence.

Core Idea
Influence is on particular existence, not essence or absolute existence.

The second doubt attempts to prove that the possible does not need an influencer by arguing that influence is impossible in all respects. This passage presents that argument and then offers the crucial answer: influence is on the particular existence of each thing, which is the contingent aspect that needs a cause. Let's unpack the three aspects and see why the answer resolves the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the structure: the doubt states three impossible cases, then the answer.
The doubt uses 'إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به'.
2
Identify the three aspects of influence
Find the three categories: essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), and being qualified (mawsufiyya). Note why each is deemed impossible for influence.
Essence cannot be made itself; existence is a state; being qualified is non-existential.
3
Understand the logic of the doubt
The doubt argues that if influence is impossible in all three, then the possible is independent. See how each impossibility is argued.
This relies on earlier discussion about making the essence.
4
Contrast with the answer
The answer redirects influence to 'particular existence' (al-wujud al-khas). Understand why this avoids the earlier impossibilities.
Reference to earlier discussion that al-maj'ul is al-wujud al-khas.
5
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on how this answer preserves the need for an influencer for contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثانية) : وهي أيضاً دالة على أن الممكن غير محتاج إلى مؤثر، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره؛ إذ ذلك فرع إمكان التأثير، وهو محال إذ ( التأثير إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن التأثير في شيء من هذه الثلاثة كانت الماهية الموجودة مستغنية عما فرض مؤثراً بالقياس إليها ، ( وقد بطلت ) هذه الأقسام كلها فيما مر؛ لأن جعل الماهية تلك الماهية محال، وكذا جعل الوجود وجوداً ، وأيضاً . حال فلا يقبل تأثيراً ، والموصوفية عدمية فلا تكون أثراً ) والجواب : أنه ) أي التأثير (في الوجود الخاص، أي) في الهويات كما مر من أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص
(The second shubha): And it also indicates that the possible is not in need of an influencer, neither for its possibility nor for anything else, because that is a branch of the possibility of influence, and it is impossible, since influence is either in the essence (mahiyya) or existence (wujud) or being qualified (mawsufiyya) by it; because if influence is not in any of these three, then the existing essence would be independent of what is assumed to be an influencer with respect to it. And all these divisions have been invalidated in what has passed, because making the essence that essence is impossible, and likewise making existence existence, and also because it (existence) is a state (hal) and thus does not accept influence, and being qualified is non-existential (adamiyya) and thus is not an effect. And the answer: that (influence) is in the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e. in the identities (al-hawiyyat), as has passed that the made thing (al-maj'ul) is the particular existence.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the second shubha (doubt) against the need for an influencer (mu'aththir) for the possible (al-mumkin). The shubha argues that the possible is not in need of any influencer because influence would have to be in one of three aspects: the essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), or being qualified (mawsufiyya) by existence. All three are claimed to be impossible: making the essence itself is impossible (as it would be making something itself), making existence is also impossible (since existence is a state (hal) and cannot receive influence), and being qualified is a non-existential (adami) attribute and thus cannot be an effect. Therefore, influence is impossible, so the possible does not require an influencer. The answer (al-jawab) to this shubha is that influence is in the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., the individual identities (al-hawiyyat). This aligns with the earlier discussion that the made thing (al-maj'ul) is the particular existence, not the essence or absolute existence. Thus, influence is not in the essence (which is not made) nor in the absolute existence (which is a state), but in the concrete, particular existence that distinguishes each individual entity.

Key Terms
الماهية (mahiyya)
الوجود (wujud)
الموصوفية (mawsufiyya)
الوجود الخاص (al-wujud al-khas)
الهويات (al-hawiyyat)
المجعول (al-maj'ul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that influence is on the essence itself.
Correction: The answer clarifies that influence is on the particular existence, which is the contingent aspect, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second shubha, why is influence in the essence (mahiyya) impossible?
A) Because the essence is a non-existential attribute.
B) Because making the essence that essence would be a tautology and impossible.
C) Because the essence does not exist.
D) Because influence only occurs in existence.

Key Takeaway
Influence is on particular existence, not on essence or absolute existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your very existence, in its particularity, is a direct act of Allah's influence, or do you take it for granted as a natural given? How does the concept of 'particular existence' as the object of divine influence change how you view your own identity and purpose?

When you reflect on your own essence and existence, do you realize that neither your essence nor your existence in general is directly made by you, but your particular being is constantly sustained by Allah? The shubha argued that influence is impossible because it cannot affect essence or existence. But Allah's influence is on your particular existence. The Second Shubha: Where Does the Effecter Act?
Al-Jurjani presents a shubha on the impossibility of effect and answers by specifying effect in specific existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second shubha attempts to deny the need for a cause, and how the Ash‘arī answer locates the effecter's action in specific existence.

Core Idea
The effecter acts not on essence or absolute existence, but on specific existence.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection: if effect is impossible in essence, existence, or being-described, then the possible needs no cause. Al-Jurjani then offers the Ash‘arī response: effect is in specific existence, the individual identity of the thing. Understanding this distinction is key to the Ash‘arī theory of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two parts: the objection (shubha) and the answer.
The shubha is structured as a trilemma.
2
Identify the three impossible loci
The shubha claims effect must be in essence, existence, or being-described. For each, the passage gives a reason why it's impossible. List these reasons.
Essence: making it itself is circular; Existence: a state (hāl) cannot receive effect; Being-described: non-existent.
3
Understand the answer
The answer specifies that effect is in specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), not the absolute existence or essence. The commentary clarifies that this means consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘) of the individual identity.
This avoids the objections because specific existence is not a mere state, but the concrete particularized existence of a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثانية) : وهي أيضاً دالة على أن الممكن غير محتاج إلى مؤثر، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره؛ إذ ذلك فرع إمكان التأثير، وهو محال إذ ( التأثير إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن التأثير في شيء من هذه الثلاثة كانت الماهية الموجودة مستغنية عما فرض مؤثراً بالقياس إليها ، ( وقد بطلت ) هذه الأقسام كلها فيما مر؛ لأن جعل الماهية تلك الماهية محال، وكذا جعل الوجود وجوداً ، وأيضاً . حال فلا يقبل تأثيراً ، والموصوفية عدمية فلا تكون أثراً ) والجواب : أنه ) أي التأثير (في الوجود الخاص، أي) في الهويات كما مر من أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص
. قوله : ( أي التأثير في الوجود الخاص) أي التأثير في الوجودات الخاصة التي هي الهويات، أعني الماهيات الشخصية بناء على رأي الشيخ الأشعري. ومعنى التأثير الاستتباع لا في جعل الماهية ماهية حتى يقال أنه لا يمكن توسط الجعل بين الشيء ونفسه ولا في الموصوفية التي اعتبارية، ولا في الوجود المطلق الذي زعمتموه أنه حال .
(The second [shubha]): And it also indicates that the possible is not in need of an effecter, neither for its possibility nor for anything else; because that [need] is a branch of the possibility of effect, and it is impossible since (effect is either in the essence, or existence, or being described by it; because if effect is not in any of these three, then the existing essence would be independent of what is assumed to be an effecter in relation to it, and these divisions are all invalid as has passed; because making the essence that essence is impossible, and likewise making existence existence, and also [because] a state (hāl) does not receive effect, and being described is non-existent so it cannot be an effect). And the answer: that [effect] is in specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), i.e., in the identities (al-hawiyyāt) as has passed that what is made is specific existence. His statement: (i.e., effect in specific existence) i.e., effect in the particular existences which are the identities, meaning the individual essences according to the view of Shaykh al-Ash‘arī. And the meaning of effect is consequential bringing-into-existence (al-istitbā‘), not in making the essence an essence such that it be said that making cannot mediate between a thing and itself, nor in the being-described which is mental consideration (i‘tibāriyya), nor in the absolute existence which you claimed is a state (hāl).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the second shubha (sophistical objection) regarding the need of the possible for a cause, specifically the impossibility of effect (ta’thīr). The shubha argues that the possible does not need an effecter because effect is impossible: it would have to be either in the essence (making it that essence, impossible), or in existence (making it existent, but existence is a state (hāl) that does not receive effect), or in being described (which is non-existent and thus cannot be an effect). Since all are impossible, the possible is independent of any effecter. The answer given is that effect is in the specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ)—the particularized existence that is the identity of the thing (hawiyya)—not in the essence or absolute existence. The commentary clarifies that, according to Ash‘arī, the effect is consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘) of the specific existence, not making the essence an essence (which would be circular) nor in the mental attribute of being-described nor in the abstract existence (which is a state, hāl). Thus the objection is deflected by specifying the locus of effect.

Key Terms
شبهة
مؤثر
ماهية
وجود
موصوفية
وجود خاص
هويات
استتباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies the reality of effect altogether.
Correction: The answer does not deny effect; it relocates it to specific existence, affirming that the effecter brings the thing into existence as a particular individual, not by acting on the essence or absolute existence.

Checkpoint

A) In the essence (māhiyya)
B) In existence (wujūd) absolutely
C) In specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ)
D) In being described (al-mawsūfiyya)

Key Takeaway
The effecter's effect is in the specific existence of a thing, not its essence or absolute existence, understood as consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘).

Objection from Hadath of Sensible Attributes and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents an objection that the previous argument would negate hadath of attributes and answers by noting the extra nature of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani responds to the objection that his earlier argument would negate the temporal origination of sensible attributes by clarifying that the argument applies only to existence, not the essences.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the proof targets existence, not quiddity.

Al-Jurjani considers a potent counterexample: sensible attributes like heat and cold clearly arise from their causes. If his earlier reasoning—that effect in essence, existence, or description is impossible—were taken universally, it would deny this obvious fact. He then provides the answer by referencing the earlier investigation (taḥqīq) that existence is superadded to essence. This restricts the proof to the existence of attributes, leaving their essences untouched.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the counterexample: sensible attributes are originated, so the earlier argument would incorrectly deny their hadath.
2
Identify the three possible loci of effect
The objection says effect must be either in essence, existence, or being described by it, and all are impossible per the earlier proof. Understand why each is considered impossible.
3
Understand the answer's key distinction
The answer points out that the earlier proof assumed existence is extra to essence. Thus it only applies to the existence of attributes, not their essences. The objection fails because it tries to apply the proof to essences.
Essences are not 'made' in the sense of causation.
4
Grasp the importance of the restriction
Al-Jurjani explicitly includes the phrase 'from the one who originates them' to limit the argument's scope to the act of granting existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فينفي ما ذكرتموه ( الحدوث ) أي حدوث الصفات المحسوسة عمن يحدثها؛ لأن تأثيره إما في ماهيتها أو وجودها أو موصوفيتها به والكل باطل لما ذكرتم بعينه.
قوله : ( وقد سبق منا إلخ) التحقيق السابق مبني على كون الوجود زائداً على الماهية كما مر . قوله : ( عمن يحدثها اعتبر هذا التقييد ليكون الدليل المذكور جارياً في صورة النقض، ولولا اعتباره لا يمكن إجراؤه ، إذ لو قيل بحدوث ماهياتها لا يمكن إبطاله بأن جعل الماهية محال إذ لا جعل .
And also it negates what you mentioned (al-ḥudūth), i.e., the temporal origination of sensible attributes from the one who originates them; because His effect is either in their essence, or existence, or being described by it, and all are invalid according to what you mentioned exactly. His statement: (And there has preceded from us [the investigation]) the preceding investigation is based on existence being superadded to essence (zā’id ‘alā al-māhiyya) as has passed. His statement: (from the one who originates them) consider this restriction in order for the mentioned proof to apply in the case of the counterexample; and were it not for its consideration, it could not be applied, because if it were said that their essences are originated, it cannot be refuted on the grounds that making the essence [into that essence] is impossible, since there is no making.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (naqḍ) based on the undeniable fact that sensible attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maḥsūsa) come into existence from an originator (e.g., heat from fire). If the previous reasoning—that effect in essence/existence/description is impossible—were correct, it would also negate this obvious hadath (temporal origination). The answer, provided in the commentary, states that the earlier investigation (taḥqīq) about effect being in specific existence presupposes that existence is superadded (zā’id) to essence (i.e., an accident). Thus, when the objector speaks of the hadath of the quiddities themselves (not their existence), the refutation does not apply because no 'making' of essence is involved—essences are not made. The restriction 'from the one who originates them' ensures the argument targets the act of causing existence, not the essence per se.

Misconception: The earlier argument implies that sensible attributes cannot originate at all.
Correction: The argument only covers the case where existence is superadded to essence; when we speak of the hadath of attributes, we are referring to their existence, not their quiddity. So the argument does not deny the obvious hadath of sensible attributes.

Key Takeaway
The argument about effect in essence/existence/description does not negate the hadath of sensible attributes because it concerns only their existence (which is superadded to essence), not their essences.

The Third Shubha: Infinite Regress of Need and Effectivity
Al-Jurjani presents the third shubha on infinite regress of need and effectivity and the answer denying their real existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third shubha attempts to prove that possible beings do not need a cause, and why the answer denies that need and effectivity are real external attributes.

Core Idea
Need and effectivity are not real entities; they are relational descriptions.

Al-Jurjani now presents the third shubha, which targets the very notions of need and effectivity. The opponent argues that if these were real attributes, they would cause an infinite regress; if they are not, then possible beings cannot truly need a cause. The answer, however, distinguishes between real existents and mere relative descriptions. This lesson unpacks that argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the shubha carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the shubha and its translation. Notice the two-pronged argument: either need/effectivity exist as real attributes (leading to regress) or they don't (so possible beings have no real need).
Focus on the logical structure: if real → regress; if not real → no need.
2
Identify the answer's key move
The answer denies the initial assumption that need and effectivity are real existents. Instead, they are merely relational. This blocks the infinite regress because only real attributes require further attributes.
The commentary clarifies that need itself would need an effecter if it were real, but since it's not, the argument dissolves.
3
Distinguish between 'no need' and 'need is non-existent'
The answer explains that the opponent conflates 'he has no need' with 'his need is non-existent.' The former negates the attribute, the latter is a positive claim about non-existence. The correct reading is that possible beings are not described as needing a cause in the sense of a real need.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding the opponent's conclusion.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this shubha is important: it challenges the causal dependency of the possible on the Necessary. By showing that need and effectivity are not real, the argument preserves the possibility of causal dependence without infinite regress.
This ties back to the overall proof for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : الحاجة والمؤثرية لو وجدتا في الخارج ( تسلسل ) ) أي لزم التسلسل، وذلك لأن الحاجة لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى الموصوف بها، إذ لا يتصور قيامها بذاتها، فللحاجة حاجة أخرى فينقل الكلام إلى حاجة الحاجة، وكذا المؤثرية لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى مؤثرية أخرى ؛ إذ يستحيل كونها واجبة بذاتها، وإذا لم تكونا موجودتين لم يكن الممكن متصفاً بالحاجة إلى سبب، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره، ولم يكن شيء متصفاً بالمؤثرية في الممكن أصلاً ، وهو المطلوب . ( والجواب :
قوله : ( لاحتاجت إلخ) ولك أن تقول : لاحتاجت إلى مؤثر إذ يستحيل كونها واجبة لذاتها، إلا أن الطريق المذكور لما كان أظهر اكتفى به . قوله : ( إذ يستحيل إلخ لامتناع تعدد الواجب ولقيامها بالغير. قوله : ( وإذا لم تكونا إلخ) بناء على عدم الفرق بين قولنا : لا حاجة له، وحاجته لا، وكذا بين قولنا : لا مؤثرية له ومؤثريته لا، كما مر .
The third shubha: Need (al-ḥāja) and effectivity (al-mu’aththiriyya), if they existed externally, (lead to infinite regress), i.e., infinite regress is necessary, because if need existed it would need that which is described by it, since it is inconceivable that it subsists by itself; so need has another need, and the discourse is transferred to the need of need; and likewise effectivity, if it existed, would need another effectivity, since it is impossible that it be necessary by itself; and if they do not exist, then the possible is not described as needing a cause, neither due to its possibility nor to anything else, and nothing is described as having effectivity in the possible at all—and that is what is desired. (And the answer: his statement: (it would need etc.) And you may say: it would need an effecter since it is impossible that it be necessary by itself, except that the mentioned path, since it is more apparent, sufficed. His statement: (since it is impossible etc.) because of the impossibility of multiplicity of the Necessary [Being] and because they subsist by another. His statement: (and if they do not exist etc.) based on the lack of distinction between our saying: he has no need, and: his need is non-existent, and likewise between our saying: he has no effectivity, and: his effectivity is non-existent, as has passed.)
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the third shubha concerning the possibility of need (ḥāja) and effectivity (mu’aththiriyya) as real attributes that would cause infinite regress. The shubha argues: If these were external realities, they would themselves need a substrate (or a cause), leading to an infinite chain; if they do not exist, then the possible cannot be described as needing a cause—which is what the opponent wants (i.e., to deny the need for a creator). The answer (jawāb) clarifies that need and effectivity are not positive existents; they are relative or mental considerations. The commentary explains that one could say need itself would need an effecter (since it cannot be self-necessary), but the clearer approach is to deny their real existence. The commentary also notes that inability to be described with need is not the same as having no need—the opponent conflates them. Thus, the infinite regress is avoided because these are not real attributes that require further attributes.

Correction: The answer shows that need and effectivity are not real existents; they are merely relational descriptions. If they were real, they would themselves require a cause, leading to infinite regress. By denying their reality, the regress is avoided.

Key Takeaway
The third shubha fails because need and effectivity are not real attributes—they are mere relational concepts—thus no infinite regress arises, and the possible's dependence on a cause remains intact.

What habits or attitudes have you built on the false assumption that you are less needy than others? How will you uproot that assumption starting today?

Fourth Objection: Contingency and Need in Existence
Fourth objection and its refutation regarding contingency and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against contingency necessitating a cause and how the answer refutes it.

Core Idea
Contingency does not necessitate a cause for non-existence, so the objection fails.

This lesson examines a specific objection to the kalam argument that contingency requires a cause. The objector claims that if contingency makes existence need a cause, the same should apply to non-existence. However, since non-existence cannot be an effect, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection: contingency would necessitate a cause for both existence and non-existence.
Notice the equal relation argument.
2
Identify the two cases in the answer
The answer distinguishes two scenarios: if non-existence can be an effect, the proof fails; if not, the implication is denied.
This is a key logical move.
3
Reflect on why the answer is effective
Consider how the answer preserves the need for a cause only for existence, blocking the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الرابعة : وهي مخصوصة بنفي كون الإمكان محوجاً أن يقال : (لو أحوج ) الإمكان ( في الوجود) إلى المؤثر ( لأحوج في العدم) أيضاً إلى المؤثر، (لاستواء نسبتهما إِليه أي نسبة الوجود والعدم إلى الإمكان لأنه رفع الضرورة الذاتية عنهما معاً فكما أن الوجود ممكن كذلك العدم ممكن، (لكن العدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً لشيء سواء كان عدماً أصلياً أو طارئاً، وفي الأصلي مانع آخر، وهو أنه مستمر فالتأثير فيه تحصيل للحاصل فوجب أن لا يكون الوجود أيضاً أثراً لشيء، ( والجواب : أن العدم إن صلح أثراً بطل دليلكم لبطلان انتفاء اللازم حينئذ ، ( وإلا ) وإن لم يصلح (منعنا الملازمة، أي لا نسلم أنه لو أحوج في
The fourth doubt: and it is specific to negating that contingency necessitates need. It is said: If contingency necessitated a cause in existence, it would also necessitate a cause in non-existence, because of the equal relation of both to it, i.e., the relation of existence and non-existence to contingency, since it removes essential necessity from both. So just as existence is possible, so too non-existence is possible. But non-existence is pure negation, not fit to be an effect of anything, whether original or accidental non-existence. In original non-existence, there is another obstacle: it is continuous, so effecting it would be obtaining what is already obtained. Hence existence must also not be an effect of anything. The answer: That non-existence, if it is fit to be an effect, then your proof fails due to the falsity of the necessary not occurring then. And if not, we deny the implication, i.e., we do not accept that it necessitates a cause in non-existence.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection (shubha rabi'a) specifically targeting the claim that contingency (imkan) necessitates a cause. The objector argues: if contingency makes existence need a cause, then the same should apply to non-existence because contingency is equally related to both (it removes essential necessity from both). But non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect, so existence should not be an effect either. The answer distinguishes two cases: if non-existence can be an effect, then the objector's proof fails because the necessary consequence (that existence also has no cause) does not hold; if non-existence cannot be an effect, then we deny the implication (that contingency necessitates a cause in non-existence). Thus, the objection is refuted.

Misconception: The objection is valid because non-existence cannot be an effect, so existence also should not be an effect.
Correction: The answer denies the implication when non-existence cannot be an effect, so the objection does not force the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The answer to the fourth objection shows that contingency does not necessitate a cause for non-existence, preserving the need for a cause only for existence.

Objection and Response on Non-Existence as an Effect
Al-Jurjani defends the claim that non-existence can be an effect of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the claim that non-existence can be an effect of non-existence, and how he uses rational necessity to refute the objection that this would lead to allowing existence from non-existence.

Core Idea
Rational necessity distinguishes non-existence from non-existence and existence from non-existence.

Al-Jurjani presents a sophisticated argument in defense of the idea that non-existence can be an effect (maʿlūl). The objector claims that if non-existence can be attributed to non-existence, then existence could also be attributed to non-existence, which would undermine the proof for God's existence. Al-Jurjani counters by appealing to rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) as the decisive factor that permits one and prohibits the other. In this lesson, we will examine this exchange and see how the author upholds the logical integrity of his position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that this is an objection-response argument within a larger discussion on contingency and causality. The issue is whether non-existence can be an effect.
Recall that in kalam, effects depend on causes.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if non-existence can be an effect, then existence could also come from non-existence, which would negate the need for a creator and close the door to proving God's existence.
3
Examine al-Jurjani's response
He denies the implication, stating that rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) judges the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence and the impossibility of existence from non-existence.
Rational necessity is a self-evident truth.
4
Reflect on the role of rational necessity
Consider why the author appeals to innate rational judgment rather than further logical deduction. It serves as a foundational principle that stops the objection.
5
Apply the lesson
See how this argument fits into the broader proof for God's existence. The ability to distinguish between types of causal dependence is crucial.

The third observation - the fourth purpose: On investigations of the contingent per se.
Existence is more in need than non-existence due to the clear difference: existence can be an effect while non-existence cannot. So possibility makes necessary [need] in the aspect that can be an effect, and it does not follow that it makes necessary [need] in the aspect that cannot be an effect at all. And we say: from the start without hesitation: if we grant the aforementioned implication in your proof, we do not grant that non-existence cannot be an effect of anything, i.e., we do not grant the falsity of the consequent. For the non-existence of the effect according to us is due to the non-existence of the cause. For if the cause were not non-existent, the effect would not be non-existent. It cannot be said: if it is permissible to attribute non-existence to it (i.e., to attribute non-existence as you mentioned of the non-existence of the effect to the non-existence of the cause), then it would also be permissible to attribute existence to it (i.e., to non-existence). And that (i.e., the permissibility of attributing existence to non-existence) negates the need for the existence of the efficient cause in the world, thus closing the door to proving the existence of the Creator. For we say: this is a statement about the chain. Moreover, the implication is denied. For rational necessity judges the permissibility of that (i.e., attributing non-existence to non-existence) and the impossibility of this (i.e., attributing existence to non-existence), so that implication is not valid at all. The fifth doubt, and it is also specific to negating...
[Commentary begins]
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response in a kalam argument about whether non-existence (ʿadam) can be an effect (athar). The author (likely al-Jurjani) begins by stating the clear difference: existence can be an effect, non-existence cannot. From this, he argues that possibility (imkān) necessitates need only in the aspect that can be an effect. The objector then argues: if we grant the implication (mulāzama) that non-existence cannot be an effect, the opponent might claim that non-existence of an effect is due to non-existence of the cause. But the objector retorts: if that were allowed, then existence could also be attributed to non-existence, which would eliminate the need for an efficient cause (muʾaththir) and thus destroy the proof for God's existence. The response is that this objection is mistaken because rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) distinguishes between the two cases: non-existence can be an effect of non-existence, but existence cannot be an effect of non-existence. The implication is denied. The commentary (sharh) then clarifies each part of the text, explaining that the author's statement 'we deny the implication' does not negate the truth of the consequent, and that the author's phrasing 'from the start without hesitation' is meant to avoid an apparent contradiction in the text.

وجود (wujūd, existence)
عدم (ʿadam, non-existence)
إمكان (imkān, possibility)
علة (ʿilla, cause)
معلول (maʿlūl, effect)
ملازمة (mulāzama, implication/necessary connection)
ضرورة عقلية (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya, rational necessity)
مؤثر (muʾaththir, efficient cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If non-existence can be an effect, then existence could also come from non-existence.
Correction: Rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) judges the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence but the impossibility of existence from non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the objection that allowing non-existence as an effect would also allow existence to come from non-existence?
A) Because the objector has misapplied the principle of implication
B) Because rational necessity judges the permissibility of one and the impossibility of the other
C) Because existence is more perfect than non-existence
D) Because the chain of causation would be broken

Key Takeaway
Rational necessity is the deciding factor that distinguishes between the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence and the impossibility of existence from non-existence, preserving the proof for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith in a Creator is logically fragile—that a single philosophical objection could dismantle it? After studying this passage, do you see that the proof remains strong because rational necessity itself protects it? What would it mean for your certainty if you hadn't encountered this distinction?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you take it as self-evident that you need a Cause? But the passage says non-existence does not need a cause—yet your existence once was non-existence. Does that unsettle you? How do you reconcile that your coming into being required a Creator, while the state before you existed did not?
?
Imagine you are arguing with someone who says, 'If God is needed for existence, then who created God?' They are committing the same error as the objector here—treating existence and non-existence as symmetrical. How does this lesson equip you to answer that? But more importantly, does your heart truly rest in the rational necessity that God is the uncaused cause, or do you still harbor intellectual doubts that you haven't faced?

The Fifth Doubt: Does Possibility Require a Cause for Survival?
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the doubt that possibility necessitates need for a cause during survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that possibility would necessitate need for a cause during survival and why this leads to absurdities such as obtaining the already obtained.

Core Idea
Possibility alone cannot necessitate a cause during survival, or else it would require obtaining the already obtained.

In this lesson, al-Jurjani presents the fifth doubt concerning whether possibility (imkān) necessitates need for an efficient cause during the state of survival. The doubt argues that if possibility is the real ground for need, then since it remains with the contingent after origination, need should persist. But this leads to absurd consequences. We will explore the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the doubt and its refutation. Note the structure: doubt, then response showing two absurd outcomes.
The passage uses a conditional and two cases in the refutation.
2
Identify the two branches of the refutation
The refutation shows that the cause in survival either produces the same existence (obtaining the obtained) or produces a renewed thing (making it not a cause of the remaining entity). Compare these two.
These two outcomes are both impossible, so the original claim must be false.
3
Understand why possibility is said to remain
The doubt asserts that possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya) and never leaves it, so it is present in survival as well.
This is a key premise: possibility is not an accidental attribute but intrinsic.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'obtaining the already obtained'
If the cause in survival brings existence itself, but existence already exists, that is a logical absurdity (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). Consider why this is impossible.
This is a standard principle in kalam and philosophy.

الإمكان محوجاً ، ( لو كان المحوج ) إلى المؤثر ( هو الإمكان لأحوج إليه أيضاً (حال البقاء، لثبوته حينئذ أي ثبوت الإمكان للممكن في حال البقاء : ( فإنه لازم للماهية ) الممكنة تقتضيه ذاتها من حيث هي هي. ، فلا ينفك عنها أصلاً كالوجوب والامتناع الذاتيين، وإذا كان الإمكان ثابتاً حال البقاء كان معلوله الذي هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر ثابتاً أيضاً . ) والثاني باطل لأن الحاصل به أي بتأثير المؤثر حال البقاء ( إن كان نفس الوجود وأنه حاصل قبله ) أي قبل البقاء ( لزم تحصيل الحاصل وإن كان الحاصل به ( أمراً متجدداً لم يكن ) ذلك المؤثر بتأثيره ( موجباً للباقي ) الذي هو المتصف بذلك الوجود الحاصل قبل البقاء، (بل) موجباً ( لأمر آخر)، فلا يكون مؤثراً في الباقي،
قوله: (وهي أيضاً مخصوصة إلخ) هذا مبني على ما سيجيء من أن القائلين بعلية الحدوث يقولون بأن الماهية إذا حدثت أي خرجت إلى الوجود لم يبق لها الحاجة، وأما بالنظر إلى التحقيق من أن الاتصاف بالحدوث حال البقاء أيضاً لأنه عبارة عن المسبوقية فالشبهة تنفي علية الحدوث للحاجة أيضاً، كما لا يخفى .

قوله : (تقتضي من ذاتها حيث هي هي فيه بحيث إذ قد سبق أن الإمكان من المعقولات الثانية، الذي يقتضيها ذات المعقول الأول بحسب الوجود ،والذهن وقوله : من حيث هي هي يدل على أنه من لوازم الماهية بالمعنى المتعارف ثم إنه مما لا يحتاج إلى إلزامه أجزاء الشبهة إذ يكفي أن يقال : المجموع على القول بأن الإمكان هو كون الشيء بحيث لو وجه في الذهن كان متصفا بمساواة الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذاته وهذه الحيثية ثابتة له حال البقاء. اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا سبق كلام صاحب الشبهة على المشهور، وإن لم يكن مختاراً، كما يفهم من في حواشي حكمة العين أيضاً .

کلامه
The third observation - the fourth purpose: On investigations of the contingent per se.
Possibility necessitating need: [The argument is that] if the necessitator of need for the efficient cause were possibility, it would necessitate need also in the state of survival, because it is established then, i.e., the establishment of possibility for the contingent in the state of survival. For it is necessary for the possible essence; its essence requires it qua itself, so it is never separate from it, like intrinsic necessity and impossibility. And if possibility is established in the state of survival, then its effect, which is need for the efficient cause, is also established. And the second [consequent] is false because what is obtained by it, i.e., by the effect of the efficient cause in the state of survival, if it is existence itself and it is obtained before it (i.e., before survival), then it would be obtaining the obtained; if what is obtained by it is a renewed thing, then that efficient cause by its effect would not be the cause of the remaining thing (which is qualified by that existence obtained before survival), but rather a cause of something else, so it would not be an effect in the remaining thing.
[Commentary begins]
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth doubt (al-shubha al-khāmisah) concerning whether possibility (imkān) necessitates need for an efficient cause (muʾaththir) during the state of survival (ḥāl al-baqāʾ) after creation. The argument (attributed to the opponent) is: if possibility is what necessitates need, then since possibility remains with the contingent in survival (because it is a necessary concomitant of the essence, like necessity and impossibility), the need for a cause should persist as well. The response is that this leads to absurdity: either the cause in survival produces the same existence already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or produces a renewed thing, in which case it is not a cause of the remaining existent. The commentary clarifies nuances: the claim that possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence is based on the fact that possibility is one of the second intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) that are abstracted by the mind; the relation of needing a cause in survival may be affected by other conditions. The passage sets up the discussion for the refutation of the doubt.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
مؤثر (muʾaththir)
معلول (maʿlūl)
لازم الماهية (lāzim al-māhiyya)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must always necessitate need for a cause, even during survival.
Correction: The need for a cause is only for origination; during survival the cause's effect would be either redundant or unrelated, as the passage shows.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth doubt, why does possibility necessitate need for a cause even during survival?
A) Because survival is a new attribute requiring a cause
B) Because possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence and remains with it
C) Because the cause must continuously create existence
D) Because the contingent is always in need of preservation

Key Takeaway
Possibility does not necessitate need for a cause during survival, because that would lead to absurdities such as obtaining the already obtained or making the cause irrelevant to the surviving entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your continued existence at this very moment does not automatically require a cause because possibility alone doesn't necessitate it, then why do you still feel the need for God's constant support? Does your heart truly depend on Him for each breath, or do you take survival for granted?
?
The argument shows that a cause cannot produce the same existence twice—it would be obtaining the obtained. How does this challenge your understanding of God's ongoing creation? Do you recognize that every moment you exist is a fresh act of divine will, not a mere continuation?
?
If need for a cause is only for origination, not survival, then what does that say about your gratitude for being brought into existence? Are you living as if the initial act of creation is enough, or does each moment call for a renewed sense of thankfulness and reliance?

Causality and Created Things

The Persistence of Possibility and the Need for a Cause
The objection that possibility necessitates need even in persistence and the initial dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if possibility necessitates need, then need persists, and the initial dilemma that the cause's effect in persistence cannot be the same existence without absurdity.

Core Idea
Possibility remains in persistence, so need for a cause remains.

This passage presents a key objection against the view that possibility alone necessitates need. The objection argues that since possibility persists, the need must persist, leading to a dilemma: either the cause's effect in persistence is the existence itself (which is already acquired) or a new thing (which would not affect the original entity). Let's examine this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the structure of the objection: possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence, so it persists in the state of persistence, therefore need persists.
Notice the parallel with essential necessity and impossibility.
2
Identify the two consequences
The passage presents two possibilities for the cause's effect in persistence: either it is the existence itself (leading to acquisition of the acquired) or a renewed thing (leading to the cause not affecting the remaining entity). Identify each and understand why each is problematic.
3
Understand the dilemma
Ask yourself: why does the commentator consider both options impossible? The first leads to a logical absurdity (obtaining what is already obtained), the second severs the cause's connection to the remaining entity.
This dilemma sets up the need for a more refined answer later.
